  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dcfcf2e999bb7fc9bd1e7a980ef514d1cfdeace6" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:8.466ex; height:2.676ex;" alt="M - M&#039;"/></span>, the form of interest-bearing capital. [...] The thing (money, commodity, value) is now capital even as a mere thing, and capital appears as a mere thing. The result of the entire process of reproduction appears as a property inherent in the thing itself. It depends on the owner of the money, i.e., of the commodity in its continually exchangeable form, whether he wants to spend it as money or loan it out as capital. In interest-bearing capital, therefore, this automatic fetish, self-expanding value, money generating money, are brought out in their pure state and in this form it no longer bears the birth-marks of its origin. The <a href="/wiki/Social_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Social relations">social relation</a> is consummated in the relation of a thing, of money, to itself. Instead of the actual transformation of money into capital, we see here only form without content.</p><div class="templatequotecite">â€”&#8201;<cite><i>Das Kapital</i>, vol. 1, ch. 24</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wage_labour">Wage labour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Wage labour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wage_labour" title="Wage labour">Wage labour</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Worker_9.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Worker_9.JPG/220px-Worker_9.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Worker_9.JPG/330px-Worker_9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Worker_9.JPG/440px-Worker_9.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1195" data-file-height="927" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Worker_9.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An industrial <a href="/wiki/Worker" class="mw-redirect" title="Worker">worker</a> amidst heavy steel <a href="/w/index.php?title=Semi-product&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Semi-product (page does not exist)">semi-products</a> (Kinex Bearings, <a href="/wiki/Byt%C4%8Da" title="BytÄa">BytÄa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, c. 1995â€“2000)</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Wage_labour" title="Wage labour">Wage labour</a> refers to the sale of <a href="/wiki/Labour_economics" title="Labour economics">labour</a> under a formal or informal <a href="/wiki/Employment_contract" title="Employment contract">employment contract</a> to an <a href="/wiki/Employer" class="mw-redirect" title="Employer">employer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> These transactions usually occur in a <a href="/wiki/Labour_market" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour market">labour market</a> where <a href="/wiki/Wage" title="Wage">wages</a> are market determined.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Individuals who possess and supply financial capital or labor to productive ventures often become owners, either jointly (as <a href="/wiki/Shareholder" title="Shareholder">shareholders</a>) or individually. In Marxist economics, these owners of the means of production and suppliers of capital are generally called capitalists. The description of the role of the capitalist has shifted, first referring to a useless intermediary between producers to an employer of producers and eventually came to refer to owners of the means of production.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams_1983_51_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_1983_51-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Labor_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor (economics)">Labor</a> includes all physical and mental human resources, including entrepreneurial capacity and management skills, which are needed to produce products and services. <a href="/wiki/Production_(economics)" title="Production (economics)">Production</a> is the act of making goods or services by applying <a href="/wiki/Labor_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor power">labor power</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics of the capitalist mode of production see wage labour as a major, if not defining, aspect of hierarchical industrial systems. Most opponents of the institution support <a href="/wiki/Worker_self-management" class="mw-redirect" title="Worker self-management">worker self-management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Economic_democracy" title="Economic democracy">economic democracy</a> as alternatives to both wage labour and to capitalism. While most opponents of the wage system blame the capitalist owners of the means of production for its existence, most <a href="/wiki/Anarchists" class="mw-redirect" title="Anarchists">anarchists</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Libertarian_socialists" class="mw-redirect" title="Libertarian socialists">libertarian socialists</a> also hold the state as equally responsible as it exists as a tool utilised by capitalists to subsidise themselves and protect the institution of <a href="/wiki/Privatism" title="Privatism">private ownership of the means of production</a>. As some opponents of wage labour take influence from Marxist propositions, many are opposed to <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a>, but maintain respect for <a href="/wiki/Personal_property" title="Personal property">personal property</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The most common form of wage labour currently is ordinary direct, or "full-time", employment in which a free worker sells his or her labour for an indeterminate time (from a few years to the entire career of the worker) in return for a money-wage or salary and a continuing relationship with the employer which it does not in general offer contractors or other irregular staff. However, wage labour takes many other forms and explicit as opposed to implicit (i.e. conditioned by local labour and tax law) contracts are not uncommon. Economic history shows a great variety of ways in which labour is traded and exchanged. The differences show up in the form of:
</p>
<ul><li>Employment status: a worker could be employed full-time, part-time, or on a casual basis. He or she could be employed for example temporarily for a specific project only, or on a permanent basis. Part-time wage labour could combine with part-time <a href="/wiki/Self-employment" title="Self-employment">self-employment</a>. The worker could be employed also as an <a href="/wiki/Apprenticeship" title="Apprenticeship">apprentice</a>.</li>
<li>Civil (legal) status: the worker could for example be a free citizen, an <a href="/wiki/Indentured_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured labour">indentured labourer</a>, the subject of <a href="/wiki/Forced_labour" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced labour">forced labour</a> (including some prison or army labour); a worker could be assigned by the political authorities to a task, they could be a <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">semi-slave</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serf</a> bound to the land who is hired out part of the time. So the labour might be performed on a more or less voluntary basis, or on a more or less involuntary basis, in which there are many gradations.</li>
<li>Method of payment (remuneration or <a href="/wiki/Compensation_of_employees" title="Compensation of employees">compensation</a>): the work done could be paid "in cash" (a money-wage) or "in kind" (through receiving goods and/or services), or in the form of "<a href="/wiki/Piecework" class="mw-redirect" title="Piecework">piece rates</a>" where the wage is directly dependent on how much the worker produces. In some cases, the worker might be paid in the form of credit used to buy goods and services, or in the form of <a href="/wiki/Employee_stock_option" title="Employee stock option">stock options</a> or <a href="/wiki/Share_(finance)" title="Share (finance)">shares</a> in an enterprise.</li>
<li>Method of hiring: the worker might engage in a labour-contract on his or her own initiative, or he or she might hire out their labour as part of a group. However, he or she may also hire out their labour via an intermediary (such as an employment agency) to a third party. In this case, he or she is paid by the intermediary, but works for a third party which pays the intermediary. In some cases, labour is <a href="/wiki/Subcontracting" class="mw-redirect" title="Subcontracting">subcontracted</a> several times, with several intermediaries. Another possibility is that the worker is assigned or posted to a job by a political authority, or that an agency hires out a worker to an enterprise together with the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_war">Effects of war</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: Effects of war">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City,_Kansas_(USA).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City%2C_Kansas_%28USA%29.jpg/220px-Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City%2C_Kansas_%28USA%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City%2C_Kansas_%28USA%29.jpg/330px-Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City%2C_Kansas_%28USA%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City%2C_Kansas_%28USA%29.jpg/440px-Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City%2C_Kansas_%28USA%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="7239" data-file-height="5562" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alfred_T._Palmer_-_Assembling_the_North_American_B-25_Mitchell_at_Kansas_City,_Kansas_(USA).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The common view among economic historians is that the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> ended with the advent of World War II (assembling the North American <a href="/wiki/B-25_Mitchell" class="mw-redirect" title="B-25 Mitchell">B-25 Mitchell</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Kansas City">Kansas City</a>, 1942)</div></div></div>
<p>War typically causes the diversion, destruction and creation of capital assets as capital assets are both destroyed or consumed and diverted to types of production needed to fight the war. Many assets are wasted and in some few cases created specifically to fight a war. War driven demands may be a powerful stimulus for the accumulation of capital and production capability in limited areas and market expansion outside the immediate theatre of war. Often this has induced laws against perceived and real <a href="/wiki/War_profiteering" title="War profiteering">war profiteering</a>.
</p><p>The total hours worked in the United States rose by 34 percent during World War II, even though the military draft reduced the civilian labor force by 11 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>War destruction can be illustrated by looking at <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Industrial war damage was heaviest in Japan, where 1/4 of factory buildings and 1/3 of plant and equipment were destroyed; 1/7 of electric power-generating capacity was destroyed and 6/7 of oil refining capacity. The Japanese merchant fleet lost 80% of their ships. In Germany in 1944, when air attacks were heaviest, 6.5% of machine tools were damaged or destroyed, but around 90% were later repaired. About 10% of steel production capacity was lost. In Europe, the United States and the Soviet Union enormous resources were accumulated and ultimately dissipated as planes, ships, tanks and so on were built and then lost or destroyed.
</p><p>Germany's total war damage was estimated at about 17.5% of the pre-war total capital stock by value, i.e. about 1/6. In the <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a> area alone, there were 8 million refugees lacking basic necessities. In 1945, less than 10% of the railways were still operating. 2,395 rail bridges were destroyed and a total of 7,500 bridges, 10,000 locomotives and more than 100,000 goods wagons were destroyed. Less than 40% of the remaining locomotives were operational.
</p><p>However, by the first quarter of 1946 European rail traffic, which was given assistance and preferences (by Western appointed military governors) for resources and material as an essential asset, regained its prewar operational level. At the end of the year, 90% of Germany's railway lines were operating again. In retrospect, the rapidity of infrastructure reconstruction appears astonishing.
</p><p>Initially, in May 1945 newly installed United States president <a href="/wiki/Harry_S._Truman" title="Harry S. Truman">Harry S. Truman</a>'s directive had been that no steps would be taken towards economic rehabilitation of Germany. In fact, the initial industry plan of 1946 prohibited production in excess of half of the 1938 level; the iron and steel industry was allowed to produce only less than a third of pre-war output. These plans were rapidly revised and better plans were instituted. In 1946, over 10% of Germany's physical capital stock (plant and equipment) was also dismantled and confiscated, most of it going to the Soviet Union. By 1947, industrial production in Germany was at 1/3 of the 1938 level and industrial investment at about 1/2 the 1938 level.
</p><p>The first big strike-wave in the <a href="/wiki/Ruhr" title="Ruhr">Ruhr</a> occurred in early 1947â€”it was about food rations and housing, but soon there were demands for nationalisation. However, the United States appointed military governor (Newman) stated at the time that he had the power to break strikes by withholding food rations. The clear message was "no work, no eat". As the military controls in Western Germany were nearly all relinquished and the Germans were allowed to rebuild their own economy with <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a> aid things rapidly improved. By 1951, German industrial production had overtaken the prewar level. The Marshall Aid funds were important, but after the currency reform (which permitted German capitalists to revalue their assets) and the establishment of a new political system much more important was the commitment of the United States to rebuilding German capitalism and establishing a free market economy and government, rather than keeping Germany in a weak position. Initially, average <a href="/wiki/Real_wages" title="Real wages">real wages</a> remained low, lower even than in 1938, until the early 1950s while profitability was unusually high. So the total investment fund, aided by credits, was also high, resulting in a high rate of capital accumulation which was nearly all reinvested in new construction or new tools. This was called the <a href="/wiki/German_economic_miracle" class="mw-redirect" title="German economic miracle">German economic miracle</a> or <i>Wirtschaftswunder</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In Italy, the victorious Allies did three things in 1945: they imposed their absolute military authority; they quickly disarmed the Italian partisans from a very large stock of weapons; and they agreed to a state guarantee of wage payments as well as a veto on all sackings of workers from their jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Although the Italian Communist Party grew very large immediately after the war endedâ€”it achieved a membership of 1.7 million people in a population of 45 millionâ€”it was outmaneuvered through a complicated political battle by the Christian Democrats after three years.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In the 1950s, an economic boom began in Italy, at first fueled by internal demand and then also by exports.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In modern times, it has often been possible to rebuild physical capital assets destroyed in wars completely within the space of about 10 years, except in cases of severe pollution by <a href="/wiki/Chemical_warfare" title="Chemical warfare">chemical warfare</a> or other kinds of irreparable devastation. However, damage to <a href="/wiki/Human_capital" title="Human capital">human capital</a> has been much more devastating in terms of fatalities (in the case of World War II, about 55 million deaths), permanent physical disability, enduring ethnic hostility and psychological injuries which have effects for at least several generations.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_capitalism" title="Criticism of capitalism">Criticism of capitalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png/220px-Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png/330px-Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png/440px-Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png 2x" data-file-width="2382" data-file-height="3000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anti-capitalism_color%E2%80%94_Restored.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Workers_of_the_World" title="Industrial Workers of the World">Industrial Workers of the World</a> poster "<a href="/wiki/Pyramid_of_Capitalist_System" title="Pyramid of Capitalist System">Pyramid of Capitalist System</a>" (1911)</div></div></div>
<p>Critics of capitalism associate the economic system with <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">social inequality</a>; unfair <a href="/wiki/Distribution_of_wealth" title="Distribution of wealth">distribution of wealth</a> and power; <a href="/wiki/Economic_materialism" title="Economic materialism">materialism</a>; repression of workers and <a href="/wiki/Trade_union" title="Trade union">trade unionists</a>; <a href="/wiki/Social_alienation" title="Social alienation">social alienation</a>; <a href="/wiki/Economic_inequality" title="Economic inequality">economic inequality</a>; <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a>; and economic instability. Many socialists consider capitalism to be irrational in that production and the direction of the economy are unplanned, creating many inconsistencies and internal contradictions.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Capitalism and individual property rights have been associated with the <a href="/wiki/Tragedy_of_the_anticommons" title="Tragedy of the anticommons">tragedy of the anticommons</a> where owners are unable to agree. <a href="/wiki/Marxian_economist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxian economist">Marxian economist</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_D._Wolff" title="Richard D. Wolff">Richard D. Wolff</a> postulates that capitalist economies prioritize profits and capital accumulation over the social needs of communities and capitalist enterprises rarely include the workers in the basic decisions of the enterprise.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Democratic_socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Democratic socialist">Democratic socialists</a> argue that the role of the state in a capitalist society is to defend the interests of the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-democraticsocialists_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-democraticsocialists-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> These states take actions to implement such things as unified national markets, national currencies and customs system.<sup id="cite_ref-democraticsocialists_150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-democraticsocialists-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Capitalism and capitalist governments have also been criticized as <a href="/wiki/Oligarchic" class="mw-redirect" title="Oligarchic">oligarchic</a> in nature<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> due to the inevitable inequality<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> characteristic of economic progress.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <a href="/wiki/Labor_history_(discipline)" title="Labor history (discipline)">labor historians</a> and scholars have argued that <a href="/wiki/Unfree_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Unfree labor">unfree labor</a>â€”by <a href="/wiki/Slave" class="mw-redirect" title="Slave">slaves</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indentured_servant" class="mw-redirect" title="Indentured servant">indentured servants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incarceration_in_the_United_States#Employment" title="Incarceration in the United States">prisoners</a> or other coerced personsâ€”is compatible with capitalist relations. <a href="/wiki/Tom_Brass" title="Tom Brass">Tom Brass</a> argued that unfree labor is acceptable to capital.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Historian <a href="/wiki/Greg_Grandin" title="Greg Grandin">Greg Grandin</a> argues that capitalism has its origins in slavery, saying that "[w]hen historians talk about the Atlantic market revolution, they are talking about capitalism. And when they are talking about capitalism, they are talking about slavery."<sup id="cite_ref-Grandin_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Grandin-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> Some historians, including <a href="/wiki/Edward_E._Baptist" title="Edward E. Baptist">Edward E. Baptist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sven_Beckert" title="Sven Beckert">Sven Beckert</a>, assert that slavery was an integral component in the violent development of American and global capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The Slovenian <a href="/wiki/Continental_philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental philosopher">continental philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Slavoj_%C5%BDi%C5%BEek" title="Slavoj Å½iÅ¾ek">Slavoj Å½iÅ¾ek</a> posits that the new era of global capitalism has ushered in new forms of <a href="/wiki/Slavery#Contemporary_slavery" title="Slavery">contemporary slavery</a>, including migrant workers deprived of basic civil rights on the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, the total control of workers in Asian <a href="/wiki/Sweatshops" class="mw-redirect" title="Sweatshops">sweatshops</a>, and the use of forced labor in the exploitation of natural resources in <a href="/wiki/Central_Africa" title="Central Africa">Central Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Wallerstein" title="Immanuel Wallerstein">Immanuel Wallerstein</a>, institutional racism has been "one of the most significant pillars" of the capitalist system and serves as "the ideological justification for the hierarchization of the work-force and its highly unequal distributions of reward".<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many aspects of capitalism have come under attack from the <a href="/wiki/Anti-globalization_movement" title="Anti-globalization movement">anti-globalization movement</a>, which is primarily opposed to <a href="/wiki/Corporate_capitalism" title="Corporate capitalism">corporate capitalism</a>. <a href="/wiki/Environmentalist" title="Environmentalist">Environmentalists</a> have argued that capitalism requires continual economic growth and that it will inevitably deplete the finite natural resources of Earth and cause <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">mass extinctions</a> of animal and plant life.<sup id="cite_ref-McMurty_1999_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McMurty_1999-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Such critics argue that while <a href="/wiki/Neoliberalism" title="Neoliberalism">neoliberalism</a>, the ideological backbone of contemporary globalized capitalism, has indeed increased global trade, it has also destroyed traditional ways of life, exacerbated inequality, increased global <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>, and that environmental indicators indicate massive <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a> since the late 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-DumÃ©nil_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DumÃ©nil-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png/220px-US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png/330px-US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png/440px-US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png 2x" data-file-width="728" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:US_Wealth_Inequality_-_v2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality_in_the_United_States" title="Wealth inequality in the United States">Wealth inequality</a> in the United States increased from 1989 to 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Some scholars blame the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" title="Financial crisis of 2007â€“2008">financial crisis of 2007â€“2008</a> on the neoliberal capitalist model.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Following the banking crisis of 2007, <a href="/wiki/Alan_Greenspan" title="Alan Greenspan">Alan Greenspan</a> told the United States Congress on 23 October 2008 that "[t]his modern risk-management paradigm held sway for decades. The whole intellectual edifice, however, collapsed in the summer of last year",<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> and that "I made a mistake in presuming that the self-interests of organizations, specifically banks and others, were such that they were best capable of protecting their own shareholders and their equity in firms [...] I was shocked".<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many religions have criticized or opposed specific elements of capitalism. Traditional <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> forbid <a href="/wiki/Usury" title="Usury">lending money at interest</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> although alternative methods of banking have been developed. Some Christians have criticized capitalism for its <a href="/wiki/Economic_materialism" title="Economic materialism">materialist</a> aspects and its inability to account for the wellbeing of all people.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> Many of Jesus' parables deal with economic concerns: farming, shepherding, being in debt, doing hard labor, being excluded from banquets and the houses of the rich and have implications for wealth and power distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> Catholic scholars and clergy have often criticized capitalism because of its disenfranchisement of the poor, often promoting <a href="/wiki/Distributism" title="Distributism">distributism</a> as an alternative. In his 84-page <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_exhortation" title="Apostolic exhortation">apostolic exhortation</a> <i lang="la" title="Latin language text"><a href="/wiki/Evangelii_gaudium" title="Evangelii gaudium">Evangelii gaudium</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Pope" title="Pope">Catholic Pope</a> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Francis" title="Pope Francis">Francis</a> described unfettered capitalism as "a new tyranny" and called on world leaders to fight rising poverty and inequality:<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Some people continue to defend <a href="/wiki/Trickle-down_economics" title="Trickle-down economics">trickle-down theories</a> which assume that economic growth, encouraged by a free market, will inevitably succeed in bringing about greater justice and inclusiveness in the world. This opinion, which has never been confirmed by the facts, expresses a crude and naive trust in the goodness of those wielding economic power and in the sacralized workings of the prevailing economic system. Meanwhile, the excluded are still waiting.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<p>Proponents of capitalism argue that it creates more prosperity than any other economic system and that its benefits are mainly to the ordinary person.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> Critics of capitalism variously associate it with economic instability,<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> an inability to provide for the well-being of all people<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> and an unsustainable danger to the natural environment.<sup id="cite_ref-McMurty_1999_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McMurty_1999-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Socialist" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist">Socialists</a> maintain that although capitalism is superior to all previously existing economic systems (such as feudalism or slavery), the contradiction between class interests will only be resolved by advancing into a completely new social system of production and distribution in which all persons have an equal relationship to the means of production.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term capitalism in its modern sense is often attributed to <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Das Kapital</a></i>, Marx analyzed the "<a href="/wiki/Capitalist_mode_of_production_(Marxist_theory)" title="Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)">capitalist mode of production</a>" using a method of understanding today known as <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a>. However, Marx himself rarely used the term "capitalism" while it was used twice in the more political interpretations of his work, primarily authored by his collaborator <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>. In the 20th century, defenders of the capitalist system often replaced the term capitalism with phrases such as free enterprise and private enterprise and replaced capitalist with <a href="/wiki/Rentier_capitalism" title="Rentier capitalism">rentier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Investor" title="Investor">investor</a> in reaction to the negative connotations associated with capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-Williams_1983_51_140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_1983_51-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Profit_motive_2">Profit motive</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Profit motive">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The majority of criticisms against the profit motive centre on the idea that the profit motive encourages selfishness and greed, rather than serve the public good or necessarily creating an increase in net wealth. Critics of the profit motive contend that companies disregard morals or public safety in the pursuit of profits.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">Free market</a> economists counter that the profit motive, coupled with competition, actually reduces the final price of an item for consumption, rather than raising it. They argue that businesses profit by selling a good at a lower price and at a greater volume than the competition. Economist <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Sowell" title="Thomas Sowell">Thomas Sowell</a> uses supermarkets as an example to illustrate this point: "It has been estimated that a supermarket makes a clear profit of about a penny on a dollar of sales. If that sounds pretty skimpy, remember that it is collecting that penny on every dollar at several cash registers simultaneously and, in many cases, around the clock".<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>American economist <a href="/wiki/Milton_Friedman" title="Milton Friedman">Milton Friedman</a> has argued that greed and self-interest are <a href="/wiki/Trait_theory#List_of_personality_traits" title="Trait theory">universal human traits</a>. On a 1979 episode of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Phil_Donahue_Show" title="The Phil Donahue Show">The Phil Donahue Show</a></i>, Friedman states: "The world runs on individuals pursuing their separate interests". He continues by explaining that only in capitalist countries, where individuals can pursue their own self-interest, people have been able to escape from "grinding poverty".<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_to_slavery">Comparison to slavery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Comparison to slavery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wage_slavery" title="Wage slavery">Wage slavery</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg/220px-Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg/330px-Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg/440px-Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3300" data-file-height="2234" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pinkerton_escorts_hocking_valley_leslies.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pinkerton_National_Detective_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Pinkerton National Detective Agency">Pinkerton guards</a> escort strikebreakers in <a href="/wiki/Buchtel,_Ohio" title="Buchtel, Ohio">Buchtel, Ohio</a>, 1884</div></div></div>
<p>Wage labor has long been compared to slavery.<sup id="cite_ref-English_Working_Class_p._599_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Working_Class_p._599-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-English_Working_Class_p._912_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-English_Working_Class_p._912-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Ostergaard_p._133_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Ostergaard_p._133-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Shop_Floor_p._37_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shop_Floor_p._37-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> As a result, the phrase "<a href="/wiki/Labour_economics#Wage_slavery" title="Labour economics">wage slavery</a>" is often utilized as a pejorative for wage labor.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, advocates of slavery looked upon the "comparative evils of Slave Society and of Free Society, of slavery to human Masters and slavery to Capital"<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzhugh_1857_xvi_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzhugh_1857_xvi-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> and proceeded to argue that wage slavery was actually worse than <a href="/wiki/Chattel_slavery" class="mw-redirect" title="Chattel slavery">chattel slavery</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> Slavery apologists like <a href="/wiki/George_Fitzhugh" title="George Fitzhugh">George Fitzhugh</a> contended that workers only accepted wage labor with the passage of time as they became "familiarised and inattentive to the infected social atmosphere they continually inhale".<sup id="cite_ref-Fitzhugh_1857_xvi_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fitzhugh_1857_xvi-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> Scholars have debated the exact relationship between wage labor, slavery, and capitalism at length, especially for the <a href="/wiki/Antebellum_South" title="Antebellum South">Antebellum South</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Similarities between wage labor and slavery were noted as early as <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> in Ancient Rome, such as in <i><a href="/wiki/De_Officiis" title="De Officiis">De Officiis</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> With the advent of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, thinkers such as <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Joseph_Proudhon" title="Pierre-Joseph Proudhon">Pierre-Joseph Proudhon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> elaborated the comparison between wage labor and slavery in the context of a critique of societal property not intended for active personal use<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marx_1863_c7_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marx_1863_c7-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Luddite" title="Luddite">Luddites</a> emphasized the <a href="/wiki/Dehumanisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Dehumanisation">dehumanisation</a> brought about by machines. Before the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Southern defenders of <a href="/wiki/African_American" class="mw-redirect" title="African American">African American</a> slavery invoked the concept of wage slavery to favorably compare the condition of their slaves to workers in the North.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> The United States abolished slavery during the Civil War, but labor union activists found the metaphor useful. According to <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_B._Glickman" title="Lawrence B. Glickman">Lawrence Glickman</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a> "references abounded in the labor press, and it is hard to find a speech by a labour leader without the phrase".<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="quotebox pullquote floatleft" style="width:40%;&#32;; font-size: 91%; background-color: AliceBlue;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r887775652">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â€œ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" â€ ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>
<div class="quotebox-quote quoted right-aligned" style="text-align: left;">The slave, together with his labour-power, was sold to his owner once for all. [...] The [wage] labourer, on the other hand, sells his very self, and that by fractions. [...] He [belongs] to the capitalist class; and it is for him [...] to find a buyer in this capitalist class.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup></div>
<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="text-align: right;">â€”<a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a>, analysis of the psychological implications of wage slavery goes back to the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> era. In his 1791 book <i>On the Limits of State Action</i>, <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> thinker <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_von_Humboldt" title="Wilhelm von Humboldt">Wilhelm von Humboldt</a> explained how "whatever does not spring from a man's free choice, or is only the result of instruction and guidance, does not enter into his very nature; he does not perform it with truly human energies, but merely with mechanical exactness" and so when the laborer works under external control, "we may admire what he does, but we despise what he is".<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> Both the <a href="/wiki/Milgram_experiment" title="Milgram experiment">Milgram</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stanford_prison_experiment" title="Stanford prison experiment">Stanford experiments</a> have been found useful in the psychological study of wage-based workplace relations.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Additionally, as per anthropologist <a href="/wiki/David_Graeber" title="David Graeber">David Graeber</a>, the earliest wage labor contracts we know about were in fact contracts for the rental of chattel slaves (usually the owner would receive a share of the money and the slave another, with which to maintain his or her living expenses). According to Graeber, such arrangements were quite common in New World slavery as well, whether in the United States or Brazil.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/C._L._R._James" title="C. L. R. James">C. L. R. James</a> argued in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Black_Jacobins" title="The Black Jacobins">The Black Jacobins</a></i> that most of the techniques of human organisation employed on factory workers during the Industrial Revolution were first developed on slave plantations.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coaltub.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Coaltub.png/220px-Coaltub.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="133" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/Coaltub.png 1.5x" data-file-width="282" data-file-height="171" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coaltub.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Girl pulling a coal tub in mine, from official report of the British parliamentary commission in the mid 19th century<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Anti-capitalism" title="Anti-capitalism">anti-capitalist</a> thinkers claim that the <a href="/wiki/Elite" title="Elite">elite</a> maintain wage <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> and a divided working class through their influence over the media and entertainment industry,<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> educational institutions, unjust laws, nationalist and corporate <a href="/wiki/Propaganda" title="Propaganda">propaganda</a>, pressures and incentives to internalize values serviceable to the power structure, <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">state</a> violence, fear of <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment</a><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> and a historical legacy of exploitation and profit accumulation/transfer under prior systems, which shaped the development of economic theory:
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a> noted that employers often conspire together to keep wages low:<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886047036"/></p><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The interest of the dealers... in any particular branch of trade or manufactures, is always in some respects different from, and even opposite to, that of the publicâ€¦ [They] have generally an interest to deceive and even to oppress the publicâ€¦ We rarely hear, it has been said, of the combinations of masters, though frequently of those of workmen. But whoever imagines, upon this account, that masters rarely combine, is as ignorant of the world as of the subject. Masters are always and everywhere in a sort of tacit, but constant and uniform combination, not to raise the wages of labor above their actual rateâ€¦ It is not, however, difficult to foresee which of the two parties must, upon all ordinary occasions, have the advantage in the dispute, and force the other into a compliance with their terms.
</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> made the statement that "the citizens must not live a mechanic or a mercantile life (for such a life is ignoble and inimical to virtue), nor yet must those who are to be citizens in the best state be tillers of the soil (for leisure is needed both for the development of virtue and for active participation in politics)",<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> often paraphrased as "all paid jobs absorb and degrade the mind".<sup id="cite_ref-quotationspage.com_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-quotationspage.com-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> Cicero wrote in 44 BC that "vulgar are the means of livelihood of all hired workmen whom we pay for mere manual labour, not for artistic skill; for in their case the very wage they receive is a pledge of their slavery".<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Somewhat similar criticisms have also been expressed by some proponents of liberalism, like <a href="/wiki/Henry_George" title="Henry George">Henry George</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Gesell" title="Silvio Gesell">Silvio Gesell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Paine" title="Thomas Paine">Thomas Paine</a><sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> as well as the <a href="/wiki/Distributism" title="Distributism">Distributist</a> school of thought within the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>.
</p><p>To <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchist</a> thinkers like <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Bakunin" title="Mikhail Bakunin">Mikhail Bakunin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Kropotkin" title="Peter Kropotkin">Peter Kropotkin</a>, wage slavery was a <a href="/wiki/Class_(social)" class="mw-redirect" title="Class (social)">class condition</a> in place due to the existence of <a href="/wiki/Private_property" title="Private property">private property</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">state</a>. This class situation rested primarily on:
</p>
<ol><li>The existence of property not intended for active use.</li>
<li>The concentration of ownership in few hands.</li>
<li>The lack of direct access by workers to the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a> and consumption goods.</li>
<li>The perpetuation of a <a href="/wiki/Reserve_army_of_labour" title="Reserve army of labour">reserve army of unemployed workers</a>.</li></ol>
<p>For Marxists, labor as commodity, which is how they regard wage labor,<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> provides a fundamental point of attack against capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> "It can be persuasively argued", noted one concerned philosopher, "that the conception of the worker's labour as a commodity confirms Marx's stigmatization of the wage system of private capitalism as 'wage-slavery;' that is, as an instrument of the capitalist's for reducing the worker's condition to that of a slave, if not below it".<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> That this objection is fundamental follows immediately from Marx's conclusion that wage labor is the very foundation of capitalism: "Without a class dependent on wages, the moment individuals confront each other as free persons, there can be no production of surplus value; without the production of surplus-value there can be no capitalist production, and hence no capital and no capitalist!".<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marxian_responses">Marxian responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Marxian responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</tr><tr><td class="plainlist" style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;border-top:1px solid #C00;padding-top:0.15em;border-bottom:1px solid #C00;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Business_and_economics" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal:Business and economics">Economics portal</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Marxism" class="mw-redirect" title="Portal:Marxism">Marxism portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Marxian_economics_sidebar" title="Template:Marxian economics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Marxian_economics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Marxian economics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Marxian_economics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Marx considered capitalism to be a historically specific <a href="/wiki/Mode_of_production" title="Mode of production">mode of production</a> (the way in which the productive property is owned and controlled, combined with the corresponding <a href="/wiki/Social_relations_of_production" class="mw-redirect" title="Social relations of production">social relations</a> between individuals based on their connection with the process of production).<sup id="cite_ref-Burnham_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burnham-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The "capitalistic era" according to Karl Marx dates from 16th-century merchants and small urban workshops.<sup id="cite_ref-GSGB_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GSGB-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Marx knew that wage labour existed on a modest scale for centuries before capitalist industry. For Marx, the capitalist stage of development or "<a href="/wiki/Bourgeois" class="mw-redirect" title="Bourgeois">bourgeois</a> society" represented the most advanced form of social organization to date, but he also thought that the working classes would come to power in a worldwide <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> or <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a> transformation of human society as the end of the series of first aristocratic, then capitalist and finally working class rule was reached.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Following <a href="/wiki/Adam_Smith" title="Adam Smith">Adam Smith</a>, Marx distinguished the <a href="/wiki/Use_value" title="Use value">use value</a> of commodities from their <a href="/wiki/Exchange_value" title="Exchange value">exchange value</a> in the market. According to Marx, capital is created with the purchase of commodities for the purpose of creating new commodities with an exchange value higher than the sum of the original purchases. For Marx, the use of <a href="/wiki/Labor_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Labor power">labor power</a> had itself become a commodity under capitalism and the exchange value of labor power, as reflected in the wage, is less than the value it produces for the capitalist.
</p><p>This difference in values, he argues, constitutes <a href="/wiki/Surplus_value" title="Surplus value">surplus value</a>, which the capitalists extract and accumulate. In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Das_Kapital" title="Das Kapital">Capital</a></i>, Marx argues that the <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_mode_of_production_(Marxist_theory)" title="Capitalist mode of production (Marxist theory)">capitalist mode of production</a> is distinguished by how the owners of capital extract this surplus from workersâ€”all prior class societies had extracted <a href="/wiki/Surplus_labor" class="mw-redirect" title="Surplus labor">surplus labor</a>, but capitalism was new in doing so via the sale-value of produced commodities.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> He argues that a core requirement of a capitalist society is that a large portion of the population must not possess sources of self-sustenance that would allow them to be independent and are instead forced to sell their labor for a wage.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In conjunction with his criticism of capitalism was Marx's belief that the working class, due to its relationship to the means of production and numerical superiority under capitalism, would be the driving force behind the socialist revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> This argument is intertwined with Marx' version of the <a href="/wiki/Labor_theory_of_value" title="Labor theory of value">labor theory of value</a> arguing that labor is the source of all value and thus of profit.
</p><p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Imperialism,_the_Highest_Stage_of_Capitalism" title="Imperialism, the Highest Stage of Capitalism">Imperialism, the Highest Stage of Capitalism</a></i> (1916), <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> further developed Marxist theory and argued that capitalism necessarily led to <a href="/wiki/Monopoly_capitalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Monopoly capitalism">monopoly capitalism</a> and the export of capitalâ€”which he also called "imperialism"â€”to find new markets and resources, representing the last and highest stage of capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> Some 20th century <a href="/wiki/Marxian_economics" title="Marxian economics">Marxian economists</a> consider capitalism to be a social formation where capitalist class processes dominate, but are not exclusive.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>To these thinkers, capitalist class processes are simply those in which surplus labor takes the form of surplus value, usable as capital; other tendencies for utilization of labor nonetheless exist simultaneously in existing societies where capitalist processes predominate. However, other late Marxian thinkers argue that a social formation as a whole may be classed as capitalist if capitalism is the mode by which a surplus is extracted, even if this surplus is not produced by capitalist activity as when an absolute majority of the population is engaged in non-capitalist economic activity.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <i>Limits to Capital</i> (1982), <a href="/wiki/David_Harvey" title="David Harvey">David Harvey</a> outlines an overdetermined, "spatially restless" capitalism coupled with the spatiality of crisis formation and resolution.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> Harvey used Marx's theory of crisis to aid his argument that capitalism must have its "fixes", but that we cannot predetermine what fixes will be implemented, nor in what form they will be. His work on contractions of capital accumulation and international movements of capitalist modes of production and money flows has been influential.<sup id="cite_ref-Lawson,_Victoria_2007_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lawson,_Victoria_2007-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> According to Harvey, capitalism creates the conditions for volatile and geographically uneven development <sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sociologists such as Ulrich Beck envisioned the society of risk as a new cultural value which saw risk as a commodity to be exchanged in globalized economies. This theory suggested that disasters and capitalist economy were inevitably entwined. Disasters allow the introduction of economic programs which otherwise would be rejected as well as decentralizing the class structure in production.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_on_the_environmental_sustainability_of_capitalism">Criticism on the environmental sustainability of capitalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Criticism on the environmental sustainability of capitalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg/220px-Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg/330px-Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg/440px-Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2712" data-file-height="1907" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gullfaks_Alpha.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Gullfaks_oil_field" title="Gullfaks oil field">Gullfaks oil field</a> in the North Sea. As petroleum is a <a href="/wiki/Non-renewable_resource" title="Non-renewable resource">non-renewable natural resource</a> the industry is faced with an inevitable eventual depletion of the world's oil supply.</div></div></div>
<p>Scholars argue that the capitalist approach to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_economics" title="Environmental economics">environmental economics</a> does not take into consideration the preserving of natural resources.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> Capitalism creates three ecological problems: growth, technology, and consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_54â€“73_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_54â€“73-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> The growth problem results from the nature of capitalism, as it focuses around the accumulation of Capital.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_54â€“73_248-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_54â€“73-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> &#160;The innovation of new technologies has an impact on the environmental future as they serve as a capitalist tool in which environmental technologies can result in the expansion of the system.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Consumption is focused around the capital accumulation of commodities and neglects the use-value of production.<sup id="cite_ref-Jones_54â€“73_248-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones_54â€“73-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>  &#160;
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Supply_and_demand_2">Supply and demand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Supply and demand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>At least two assumptions are necessary for the validity of the standard model: first, that supply and demand are independent; and second, that supply is "constrained by a fixed resource". If these conditions do not hold, then the <a href="/wiki/Marshallian_surplus" class="mw-redirect" title="Marshallian surplus">Marshallian</a> model cannot be sustained. <a href="/wiki/Piero_Sraffa" title="Piero Sraffa">Sraffa's</a> critique focused on the inconsistency (except in implausible circumstances) of partial equilibrium analysis and the rationale for the upward slope of the supply curve in a market for a produced consumption good.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> The notability of Sraffa's critique is also demonstrated by <a href="/wiki/Paul_A._Samuelson" class="mw-redirect" title="Paul A. Samuelson">Paul A. Samuelson</a>'s comments and engagements with it over many years, for example:
</p>
<dl><dd>What a cleaned-up version of Sraffa (1926) establishes is how nearly empty are all of Marshall's partial equilibrium boxes. To a logical purist of Wittgenstein and Sraffa class, the Marshallian partial equilibrium box of constant cost is even more empty than the box of increasing cost.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Aggregate excess demand in a market is the difference between the quantity demanded and the quantity supplied as a function of price. In the model with an upward-sloping supply curve and downward-sloping demand curve, the aggregate excess demand function only intersects the axis at one point, namely at the point where the supply and demand curves intersect. The <a href="/wiki/Sonnenschein%E2%80%93Mantel%E2%80%93Debreu_theorem" title="Sonnenscheinâ€“Mantelâ€“Debreu theorem">Sonnenscheinâ€“Mantelâ€“Debreu theorem</a> shows that the standard model cannot be rigorously derived in general from <a href="/wiki/General_equilibrium_theory" title="General equilibrium theory">general equilibrium theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The model of prices being determined by supply and demand assumes <a href="/wiki/Perfect_competition" title="Perfect competition">perfect competition</a>. However, "economists have no adequate model of how individuals and firms adjust prices in a competitive model. If all participants are price-takers by definition, then the actor who adjusts prices to eliminate excess demand is not specified".<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> Goodwin, Nelson, Ackerman and Weisskopf write:
</p>
<dl><dd>If we mistakenly confuse <a href="/wiki/Accuracy_and_precision" title="Accuracy and precision">precision with accuracy</a>, then we might be misled into thinking that an explanation expressed in precise mathematical or graphical terms is somehow more rigorous or useful than one that takes into account particulars of history, institutions or business strategy. This is not the case. Therefore, it is important not to put too much confidence in the apparent precision of supply and demand graphs. Supply and demand analysis is a useful precisely formulated conceptual tool that clever people have devised to help us gain an abstract understanding of a complex world. It does notâ€”nor should it be expected toâ€”give us in addition an accurate and complete description of any particular real world market.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Externalities">Externalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Externalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">Externality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commons" title="Commons">Commons</a></div>
<p>Market failure occurs when an <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externality</a> is present and a market will often either under-produce a product with a positive externalisation or overproduce a product that generates a negative externalisation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Air pollution, for instance, is a negative externalisation that cannot be easily incorporated into markets as the world's air is not owned and then sold for use to polluters.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> So too much pollution could be emitted and people not involved in the production pay the cost of the pollution instead of the firm that initially emitted the air pollution.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Critics of market failure theory, like <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Coase" title="Ronald Coase">Ronald Coase</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Demsetz" title="Harold Demsetz">Harold Demsetz</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_M._Buchanan" title="James M. Buchanan">James M. Buchanan</a>, argue that government programs and policies also fall short of absolute perfection.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> While all nations currently have some kind of market regulations, the desirable degree of regulation is disputed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Counter-criticism">Counter-criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Counter-criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Austrian_School">Austrian School</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Austrian School">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Austrian_School" title="Austrian School">Austrian School</a> economists have argued that capitalism can organize itself into a complex system without an external guidance or central planning mechanism. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Hayek" title="Friedrich Hayek">Friedrich Hayek</a> considered the phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Self-organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Self-organisation">self-organisation</a> as underpinning capitalism. Prices serve as a signal as to the urgent and unfilled wants of people and the opportunity to earn profits if successful, or absorb losses if resources are used poorly or left idle, gives entrepreneurs <a href="/wiki/Incentive" title="Incentive">incentive</a> to use their <a href="/wiki/Dispersed_knowledge" title="Dispersed knowledge">knowledge</a> and resources to satisfy those wants. Thus the activities of millions of people, each seeking his own interest, are coordinated.<sup id="cite_ref-walberg2003_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-walberg2003-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ayn_Rand">Ayn Rand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Capitalism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Ayn Rand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The novelist and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Ayn_Rand" title="Ayn Rand">Ayn Rand</a> made positive moral defenses of <i>laissez-faire</i> capitalism, most notably in her 1957 novel <i><a href="/wiki/Atlas_Shrugged" title="Atlas Shrugged">Atlas Shrugged</a></i> and in her 1966 collection of essays <i><a href="/wiki/Capitalism:_The_Unknown_Ideal" title="Capitalism: The Unknown Ideal">Capitalism: The Unknown Ideal</a></i>. She argued that capitalism should be supported on moral grounds, not just on the basis of practical benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup> Her ideas have had significant influence over conservative and libertarian supporters of capitalism, especially within the American <a href="/wiki/Tea_Party_movement" title="Tea Party movement">Tea Party movement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Rand defined capitalism as "a social system based on the recognition of individual rights, including property rights, in which all property is privately owned".<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> According to Rand, the role of government in a capitalist state has three broad categories of proper functions: first, the police "to protect men from criminals"; second, the armed services "to protect men from foreign invaders"; and third, the law courts "to settle disputes among men according to objective laws".<sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Rosser, Mariana V.; Rosser, J Barkley (23 July 2003). <i>Comparative Economics in a Transforming World Economy</i>. MIT Press. p.&#160;7. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-262-18234-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-262-18234-8"><bdi>978-0-262-18234-8</bdi></a>. <q>In capitalist economies, land and produced means of production (the capital stock) are owned by private individuals or groups of private individuals organized as firms.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Comparative+Economics+in+a+Transforming+World+Economy&amp;rft.pages=7&amp;rft.pub=MIT+Press&amp;rft.date=2003-07-23&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-262-18234-8&amp;rft.aulast=Rosser&amp;rft.aufirst=Mariana+V.&amp;rft.au=Rosser%2C+J+Barkley&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Chris Jenks. <i>Core Sociological Dichotomies</i>.  "Capitalism, as a mode of production, is an economic system of manufacture and exchange which is geared toward the production and sale of commodities within a market for profit, where the manufacture of commodities consists of the use of the formally free labor of workers in exchange for a wage to create commodities in which the manufacturer extracts surplus value from the labor of the workers in terms of the difference between the wages paid to the worker and the value of the commodity produced by him/her to generate that profit." London; Thousand Oaks, CA; New Delhi. Sage. p. 383.</span>
