<li id="cite_note-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-7">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Gregory and Stuart, Paul and Robert (28 February 2013). <i>The Global Economy and its Economic Systems</i>. <li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090211160820/http://www.cato.org/university/module10.html">"The Achievements of Nineteenth-Century Classical Liberalism"</a>. <li id="cite_note-DumÃ©nil-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-DumÃ©nil_22-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-DumÃ©nil_22-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation interview"><a href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Dum%C3%A9nil" title="GÃ©rard DumÃ©nil">GÃ©rard, DumÃ©nil</a> (30 March 2010). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://therealnews.com/stories/gdumenilneolib0216pt1">"The crisis of neoliberalism"</a> (Transcript). Neoliberalism, it's a new stage of capitalism which began around 1980. It began in big countries like United Kingdom and the United States. Then it was implemented in Europe, and later in Japan, and later around the world in general. So this is a new phase of capitalism.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+crisis+of+neoliberalism&amp;rft.pub=The+Real+News&amp;rft.date=2010-03-30&amp;rft.aulast=G%C3%A9rard&amp;rft.aufirst=Dum%C3%A9nil&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Ftherealnews.com%2Fstories%2Fgdumenilneolib0216pt1&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-democraticsocialists-150"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-democraticsocialists_150-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-democraticsocialists_150-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180406141728/https://dsp.org.au/node/35">"The role and character of the state under capitalism"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Socialist_Perspective" title="Democratic Socialist Perspective">Democratic Socialist Perspective</a>. 7 August 2008. <li id="cite_note-151"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-151">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Ezra_Klein" title="Ezra Klein">Klein, Ezra</a> (11 April 2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.vox.com/2014/4/11/5581272/doom-loop-oligarchy">"The Doom Loop of Oligarchy"</a>. Vox. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180406143025/https://www.vox.com/2014/4/11/5581272/doom-loop-oligarchy">Archived</a> from the original on 6 April 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>In the year's scariest economics book, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Piketty" title="Thomas Piketty">Thomas Piketty</a> argues that capitalism, left unchecked, subverts democracy by always and everywhere concentrating wealth at the tippy-top. That creates a class with so much economic power that they begin wielding tremendous political power, too. And then they use that political power to further increase their wealth, and then they use that wealth to further increase their political power, and so on. You might call this the Doom Loop of Oligarchy: wealth buys power, which buys more wealth. You can see it playing out over the last two weeks in American politics.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=The+Doom+Loop+of+Oligarchy&amp;rft.pub=Vox&amp;rft.date=2014-04-11&amp;rft.aulast=Klein&amp;rft.aufirst=Ezra&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.vox.com%2F2014%2F4%2F11%2F5581272%2Fdoom-loop-oligarchy&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-157"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-157">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Roche, Cullen (22 August 2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.pragcap.com/is-wealth-and-income-inequality-inevitable-in-a-capitalist-system/">"Is Wealth and Income Inequality Inevitable in a Capitalist System?"</a>. <i>Pragmatic Capitalism</i>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180406155201/https://www.pragcap.com/is-wealth-and-income-inequality-inevitable-in-a-capitalist-system/">Archived</a> from the original on 6 April 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>'Inevitable' is a bit of a generalization. 'Highly probable'â€”yes. The thing is, capitalism runs on profits. And profits are optimized when they're monopolized. A good capitalist will try to monopolize the means of production therefore maximizing profits. And when profits are monopolized then you are very likely to get inequality because only a handful of people own the means of production. So capitalism has a natural tendency towards monopolization because capitalists naturally want to maximize profits.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Pragmatic+Capitalism&amp;rft.atitle=Is+Wealth+and+Income+Inequality+Inevitable+in+a+Capitalist+System%3F&amp;rft.date=2014-08-22&amp;rft.aulast=Roche&amp;rft.aufirst=Cullen&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.pragcap.com%2Fis-wealth-and-income-inequality-inevitable-in-a-capitalist-system%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-Grandin-160"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Grandin_160-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation interview"><a href="/wiki/Greg_Grandin" title="Greg Grandin">Grandin, Greg</a> (1 August 2014). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jacobinmag.com/2014/08/capitalism-and-slavery-an-interview-with-greg-grandin/">"Capitalism and Slavery: An Interview with Greg Grandin"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Jacobin_(magazine)" title="Jacobin (magazine)">Jacobin</a></i> (Interview). Interviewed by Alex Gourevitch. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180406181935/https://www.jacobinmag.com/2014/08/capitalism-and-slavery-an-interview-with-greg-grandin/">Archived</a> from the original on 6 April 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>There's many ways this happens. Deceit, through <a href="/wiki/Contraband" title="Contraband">contraband</a>, is absolutely key to the expansion of slavery in South America. When historians talk about the Atlantic market revolution, they are talking about capitalism. And when they are talking about capitalism, they are talking about slavery. And when they are talking about slavery, they are talking about corruption and crime. Not in a moral sense, in that the slave system was a crime against humanity. That it was. But it was also a crime in a technical sense: probably as many enslaved Africans came into South America as contraband, to avoid taxes and other lingering restrictions, as legally.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Jacobin&amp;rft.atitle=Capitalism+and+Slavery%3A+An+Interview+with+Greg+Grandin&amp;rft.date=2014-08-01&amp;rft.aulast=Grandin&amp;rft.aufirst=Greg&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jacobinmag.com%2F2014%2F08%2Fcapitalism-and-slavery-an-interview-with-greg-grandin%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>He noted that the immense and largely unregulated business of spreading financial risk widely, through the use of exotic financial instruments called <a href="/wiki/Derivative_(finance)" title="Derivative (finance)">derivatives</a>, had gotten out of control and had added to the havoc of today's crisis. As far back as 1994, Mr. Greenspan staunchly and successfully opposed tougher regulation on derivatives. But on Thursday, he agreed that the multitrillion-dollar market for <a href="/wiki/Credit_default_swap" title="Credit default swap">credit default swaps</a>, instruments originally created to insure bond investors against the risk of default, needed to be restrained. <li id="cite_note-183"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-183">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.vatican.va/archive/ENG0015/__P8C.HTM">"Catechism of the Catholic Church: III. The Social Doctrine of the Church"</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vatican_Publishing_House" title="Vatican Publishing House">Vatican Publishing House</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180406163022/http://www.vatican.va/archive/ENG0015/__P8C.HTM">Archived</a> from the original on 6 April 2018<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>[2425] The Church has rejected the totalitarian and atheistic ideologies associated in modem times with 'communism' or 'socialism.' She has likewise refused to accept, in the practice of 'capitalism,' individualism and the absolute primacy of the law of the marketplace over human labor.206 Regulating the economy solely by centralized planning perverts the basis of social bonds; regulating it solely by the law of the marketplace fails social justice, for 'there are many human needs which cannot be satisfied by the market.'<sup>207</sup> Reasonable regulation of the marketplace and economic initiatives, in keeping with a just hierarchy of values and a view to the common good, is to be commended.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Catechism+of+the+Catholic+Church%3A+III.+The+Social+Doctrine+of+the+Church&amp;rft.pub=Vatican+Publishing+House&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.vatican.va%2Farchive%2FENG0015%2F&#95;_P8C.HTM&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>It's certainly true that Jesus was always blessing the poor, challenging the rich, mixing with despised tax-gatherers and speaking of a time when the social order is overturned and 'the last shall be first'. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 April</span> 2018</span>. <q>Friedman responds, 'What is greed? Of course none of us are greedy; it's only the other fellow who's greedy. The world runs on individuals pursuing their separate interests. ... In the only cases in which the masses have escaped from the kind of grinding poverty you're talking about ... they have had capitalism and largely free trade. ... So that the record of history is absolutely crystal clear: that there is no alternative way so far discovered of improving the lot of the ordinary people that can hold a candle to the productive activities that are unleashed by a free enterprise system.'&#160;</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Sun&amp;rft.atitle=Milton+Friedman+Answers+Phil+Donahue%27s+Charges&amp;rft.date=2007-11-12&amp;rft.aulast=Pantin&amp;rft.aufirst=Travis&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nysun.com%2Fbusiness%2Fmilton-friedman-answers-phil-donahues-charges%2F66258%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-203"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-203">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHallgrimsdottirBenoit2007">Hallgrimsdottir &amp; Benoit 2007</a>; <a href="#CITEREFRoediger2007a">Roediger 2007a</a>.<br />The term is not without its critics, as <a href="#CITEREFRoediger2007b">Roediger 2007b</a>, p.&#160;247, notes: "[T]he challenge to loose connections of wage (or white) slavery to chattel slavery was led by <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Douglass" title="Frederick Douglass">Frederick Douglass</a> and other Black, often fugitive, abolitionists. Their challenge was mercilessly concrete. Douglass, who tried out speeches in work places before giving them in halls, was far from unable to speak to or hear white workers, but he and <a href="/wiki/William_Wells_Brown" title="William Wells Brown">William Wells Brown</a> did challenge metaphors regarding white slavery sharply. They noted, for example, that their escapes from slavery had left job openings and wondered if any white workers wanted to take the jobs."</span>

Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press. pp.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.loebclassics.com/view/marcus_tullius_cicero-de_officiis/1913/pb_LCL030.153.xml">152â€“53 (XLII)</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.4159%2FDLCL.marcus_tullius_cicero-de_officiis.1913">10.4159/DLCL.marcus_tullius_cicero-de_officiis.1913</a>. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-674-99033-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-674-99033-3"><bdi>978-0-674-99033-3</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC" title="OCLC">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/902696620">902696620</a>. <a href="/wiki/Open_Library" title="Open Library">OL</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//openlibrary.org/books/OL7693830M">7693830M</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20180406221818/https://www.loebclassics.com/view/marcus_tullius_cicero-de_officiis/1913/pb_LCL030.153.xml">Archived</a> from the original on 6 April 2018. <q>XLII. Now in regard to trades and other means of livelihood, which ones are to be considered becoming to a gentleman and which ones are vulgar, we have been taught, in general, as follows. First, those means of livelihood are rejected as undesirable which incur peopleâ€™s ill-will, as those of tax-gatherers and usurers. Unbecoming to a gentleman, too, and vulgar are the means of livelihood of all hired workmen whom we pay for mere manual labour, not for artistic skill; for in their case the very wage they receive is a pledge of their slavery. Vulgar we must consider those also who buy from wholesale merchants to retail immediately; for they would get no profits without a great deal of downright lying; and verily, there is no action that is meaner than misrepresentation. And all mechanics are engaged in vulgar trades; for no workshop can have anything liberal about it. Least respectable of all are those trades which cater for sensual pleasures[.]</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Liber+I&amp;rft.btitle=De+Officiis&amp;rft.place=Cambridge%2C+MA&amp;rft.series=Loeb+Classical+Library+%5BLCL030%5D&amp;rft.pages=152-53+%28XLII%29&amp;rft.edition=Digital&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+University+Press&amp;rft.date=1913-01-01&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F902696620&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4159%2FDLCL.marcus_tullius_cicero-de_officiis.1913&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-674-99033-3&amp;rft.aulast=Cicero&amp;rft.aufirst=Marcus+Tullius&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.loebclassics.com%2Fview%2Fmarcus_tullius_cicero-de_officiis%2F1913%2Fpb_LCL030.3.xml&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACapitalism" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
